                    if (navigator.connection.saveData != true) {

                        preloadLink.rel = "preload";

                        preloadLink.crossOrigin = "";
                        document.head.insertBefore(preloadLink, document.head.firstChild);
                    }
                }
            }

<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Genetics 2010 42:10"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Riccardo Velasco and colleagues report the genome sequence of the &#39;Golden Delicious&#39; domesticated apple. These data shed new insight into the genomic events that preceded the origin of this crop. We report a high-quality draft genome sequence of the domesticated apple (Malus &#215; domestica). We show that a relatively recent (&gt;50 million years ago) genome-wide duplication (GWD) has resulted in the transition from nine ancestral chromosomes to 17 chromosomes in the Pyreae. Traces of older GWDs partly support the monophyly of the ancestral paleohexaploidy of eudicots. Phylogenetic reconstruction of Pyreae and the genus Malus, relative to major Rosaceae taxa, identified the progenitor of the cultivated apple as M. sieversii. Expansion of gene families reported to be involved in fruit development may explain formation of the pome, a Pyreae-specific false fruit that develops by proliferation of the basal part of the sepals, the receptacle. In apple, a subclade of MADS-box genes, normally involved in flower and fruit development, is expanded to include 15 members, as are other gene families involved in Rosaceae-specific metabolism, such as transport and assimilation of sorbitol."/>

content="Flanders Institute for Biotechnology (VIB), Ghent University" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.11486.3a, 0000000104788040, Department of Plant Systems Biology, Bioinformatics and             <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs2" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs2">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs2-content"><p>We report a high-quality draft genome sequence of the domesticated apple (<i>Malus</i> × <i>domestica</i>). We show that a relatively recent (&gt;50 million years ago) genome-wide duplication (GWD) has resulted in the transition from nine ancestral chromosomes to 17 chromosomes in the Pyreae. Traces of older GWDs partly support the monophyly of the ancestral paleohexaploidy of eudicots. Phylogenetic reconstruction of Pyreae and the genus <i>Malus</i>, relative to major Rosaceae taxa, identified the progenitor of the cultivated apple as <i>M. sieversii</i>. Expansion of gene families reported to be involved in fruit development may explain formation of the pome, a Pyreae-specific false fruit that develops by proliferation of the basal part of the sepals, the receptacle. In apple, a subclade of <i>MADS-box</i> genes, normally involved in flower and fruit development, is expanded to include 15 members, as are other gene families involved in Rosaceae-specific metabolism, such as transport and assimilation of sorbitol.</p></div></div></section>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>The domesticated apple (<i>Malus</i> × <i>domestica</i> Borkh., family Rosaceae, tribe Pyreae) is the main fruit crop of temperate regions of the world. Here we describe a high-quality draft genome sequence of the diploid apple cultivar 'Golden Delicious'. Domesticated apple genotypes are all highly heterozygous, imposing technical challenges in genome sequencing and assembly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Zharkikh, A. et al. Sequencing and assembly of highly heterozygous genome of Vitis vinifera L. cv Pinot Noir: problems and solutions. J. Biotechnol. 136, 38–43 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d39870e1557">1</a></sup> while allowing identification of a very large set of SNPs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Velasco, R. et al. A high quality draft consensus sequence of the genome of a heterozygous grapevine variety. PLoS ONE 2, e1326 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d39870e1561">2</a></sup>.</p><p>Rosaceae belong to the rosids, which include one-third of all flowering plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Hummer, K.E. &amp; Janick, J. Rosaceae: taxonomy, economic importance, genomics. in Genetics and Genomics of Rosaceae (eds. Folta, K.M. &amp; Gardiner, S.E.) 1–17 (Springer, New York, 2009)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d39870e1568">3</a></sup>. Whereas the haploid (<i>x</i>) chromosome numbers of most Rosaceae are 7, 8 or 9, Pyreae have a distinctive <i>x</i> = 17. Pyreae have long been considered an example of allopolyploidization between species related to extant Spiraeoideae (<i>x</i> = 9) and Amygdaleoideae (<i>x</i> = 8), although a within-lineage polyploidization event has also been hypothesized<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Evans, R.C. &amp; Campbell, C.S. The origin of the apple subfamily (Maloideae; Rosaceae) is clarified by DNA sequence data from duplicated GBSSI genes. Am. J. Bot. 89, 1478–1484 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d39870e1585">4</a></sup>.</p><p>In addition, we examine the genetic variability in Rosaceae and related taxa, comparing Pyreae species, Rosaceae tribes and two rosid families. Gene content and order of the assembled chromosomes indicate that both recent and old GWDs have occurred. We provide a model describing the evolution of the Pyreae genome, including <i>Malus</i>, and offer insights into the origin of the domesticated apple.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Sequencing, assembling and anchoring the apple genome</h3><p>Sequencing and assembly of the 'Golden Delicious' apple genome followed the whole-genome shotgun approach. Of the 16.9-fold genome coverage, 26% was provided by Sanger dye primer sequencing of paired reads, and the remaining 74% was from 454 sequencing by synthesis of paired and unpaired reads (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#MOESM4">Supplementary Table 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#MOESM4">Supplementary Note</a>). An iterative assembly approach, previously used to assemble the highly heterozygous grape genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Zharkikh, A. et al. Sequencing and assembly of highly heterozygous genome of Vitis vinifera L. cv Pinot Noir: problems and solutions. J. Biotechnol. 136, 38–43 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d39870e1613">1</a></sup>, produced 122,146 contigs, 103,076 of which were assembled into 1,629 metacontigs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#Tab1">Table 1</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#MOESM4">Supplementary Fig. 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#MOESM4">Supplementary Note</a>). The total contig length (603.9 Mb) covers about 81.3% of the apple genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#Tab1">Table 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#MOESM4">Supplementary Note</a>). Anchoring of metacontigs (598.3 Mbp, or 71.2% of genome) was based on the high-quality genetic map with 1,643 markers (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#MOESM4">Supplementary Table 2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#MOESM4">Supplementary Note</a>). In total, 17 linkage groups, or chromosomes, were reconstructed. In the genome, repetitive elements correspond to 500.7 Mb (67%; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#MOESM4">Supplementary Note</a>). The unassembled part of the genome is 98% repetitive (138.4 Mb), and the estimated genome size is 742.3 Mb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#Tab1">Table 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#MOESM4">Supplementary Note</a>). We compared repetitive elements among ten plant species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#MOESM4">Supplementary Tables 3–6</a>). Information on relevant genes and genome parameters is provided in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#Tab1">Tables 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#Tab2">2</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#MOESM4">Supplementary Figures 2–5</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#MOESM4">Supplementary Tables 7–19</a>. Comparing gene families among ten sequenced plant species revealed apple-specific subclades of genes encoding MADS-box transcription factors and overrepresented sorbitol-related genes, which may contribute to specific aspects of apple development and carbohydrate metabolism (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#Tab2">Table 2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#MOESM4">Supplementary Table 7</a>, and see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#Sec7">Discussion</a>). The 71.2% of the genomic sequences that were anchored represent the gene-rich part of the genome, which covers as many as 90.2% of the genes assigned to the chromosomes. The distribution of transposable elements and predicted genes along the linkage groups is reported in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#MOESM4">Supplementary Figure 6</a>. The total number of genes predicted for the apple genome (57,386, including some genes that may be present only in one of the two chromosomes of a pair) is the highest reported among plants so far (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#MOESM4">Supplementary Note</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Summary of genome assembly of the apple variety 'Golden Delicious'</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/ng.654/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-2"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab2" data-test="table-caption">Table 2 Comparison of the apple genome to other sequenced plant genomes</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/ng.654/tables/2"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Genome-wide duplications and the origin of the Pyreae</h3><p>Pairwise comparison of 17 apple chromosomes highlighted strong collinearity between large segments of chromosomes 3 and 11, 5 and 10, 9 and 17, and 13 and 16, and between shorter segments of chromosomes 1 and 7, 2 and 7, 2 and 15, 4 and 12, 12 and 14, 6 and 14, and 8 and 15 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#Fig1">Fig. 1a</a>). The distribution of synonymous substitution rates (<i>K</i><sub>S</sub>)—an indication of the relative age of duplication, based on the number of synonymous substitutions in the coding sequences—peaked around 0.2 for recently duplicated genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#Fig1">Fig. 1b</a>), indicating that a (recent) GWD has shaped the genome of the domesticated apple.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Genome-wide duplications in the apple genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: Genome-wide duplications in the apple genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.654/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.654/MediaObjects/41588_2010_Article_BFng654_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.654/MediaObjects/41588_2010_Article_BFng654_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1158"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Alignment of apple chromosomes shown by pairwise dot plots based on gene homology. Strong collinearity of members of chromosome doublets, or of large chromosome segments, indicates a recent GWD (red dots and bars in <b>a</b> and <b>b</b>, respectively). Unrelated chromosomes 7 and 13 were compared as a negative control. (<b>b</b>) Reconstruction of the relationships among apple chromosomes based on the most recent and the older GWD. The model derives from data in <b>a</b> for the recent GWD and from data in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#MOESM4">Supplementary Figure 6b</a> for the oldest GWD. The chromosomes ends represented at bottom right corners in <b>a</b> are marked in black in <b>b</b>. Red bars, regions of synteny that support the recent GWD. Size of chromosomes is proportional to their DNA content in megabases. Segments of chromosomes 1, 5, 6, 8, 10, 13, 14 and 15 have no syntenic counterparts. Chromosome segments predicted to be the outcome of the older duplication are highlighted with blue, green and orange. Chromosomes 1, 2, 7, 8 and 15 do not show obvious signs of the older duplications, although they may contain short blocks of genes that reveal old paleopolyploid events. Inset graphs show that <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> from the comparisons between paralogous genes has a peak at 0.2 when the recent duplication is considered, and between 1.4 and 1.6 for the older paleopolyploid events. (<b>c</b>) Distributions of protein similarities for duplicated genes in duplicated segments compared with grape (red), poplar (green) and apple (blue).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.654/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Dating of this GWD (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#MOESM4">Supplementary Note</a>) was based on the construction of penalized likelihood trees, as described previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Fawcett, J.A., Maere, S. &amp; Van de Peer, Y. Plants with double genomes might have had a better chance to survive the Cretaceous-Tertiary extinction event. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 106, 5737–5742 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d39870e2826">5</a></sup>. Given a node of grape to rosids fixed at 115 million years ago (Mya), the GWD has been dated to between 30 and 45 Mya<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Fawcett, J.A., Maere, S. &amp; Van de Peer, Y. Plants with double genomes might have had a better chance to survive the Cretaceous-Tertiary extinction event. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 106, 5737–5742 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d39870e2830">5</a></sup>. If similar rates of protein evolution are assumed for apple and poplar (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#Fig1">Fig. 1c</a>), the recent apple GWD may be as old as that of poplar, about 60 to 65 Mya<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Tuskan, G.A. et al. The genome of black cottonwood, Populus trichocarpa (Torr. &amp; Gray). Science 313, 1596–1604 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d39870e2837">6</a></sup>.</p><p>Remnants of older large-scale gene duplications or GWDs were also evident (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#MOESM4">Supplementary Fig. 7a,b</a>). Genes in these duplicated regions had average <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> values around 1.6, as expected for paleoduplication events (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#Fig1">Fig. 1b</a>). Most remnants of these older duplications are found between chromosomes 5 and 10 and chromosomes 3 and 11, between chromosomes 3 and 11 and chromosomes 4 and 12, and between chromosomes 6 and 14, 13 and 16, and 9 and 17 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#Fig1">Fig. 1a,b</a>). Chromosomes 1, 2, 7, 8 and 15 seem relatively devoid of older duplicated blocks; however, short blocks of genes showing old polyploidy events were found on all chromosomes. One region in the apple genome with an approximate size of 4 to 7 Mbp seems to be clearly present in six copies (regions in blue, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#Fig1">Fig. 1a,b</a>). Remapping those to the ancestral state reveals a triplicate structure among parts of chromosomes 9 and 17, 6 and 14 and 13 and 16. Notably, we found that these regions are collinear with chromosomes 1, 14 and 17 of grape (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>), which have been demonstrated to be homologous because of an ancient hexaploidy<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463–467 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d39870e2864">7</a></sup>. Additional chromosomal fragments that we found to be duplicated in apple (green and yellow bars in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#Fig1">Fig. 1b</a>) can also be interpreted as remains of a paleohexaploid state of the eudicot progenitor on the basis of dot-plot comparisons among other grape and apple chromosomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#MOESM4">Supplementary Fig. 8a,b</a>). This provides further evidence for a paleohexaploid state shared by most eudicots<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Tang, H. et al. Synteny and collinearity in plant genomes. Science 320, 486 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d39870e2874">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Van de Peer, Y., Fawcett, J.A., Proost, S., Sterck, L. &amp; Vandepoele, K. The flowering world: a tale of duplications. Trends Plant Sci. 14, 680–688 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d39870e2877">9</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Dot-plot comparisons between apple and grape chromosomes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: Dot-plot comparisons between apple and grape chromosomes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.654/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.654/MediaObjects/41588_2010_Article_BFng654_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.654/MediaObjects/41588_2010_Article_BFng654_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="629"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Dot plots are based on gene homology. The apple chromosomes are those with the segment triplication deriving from an old GWD (shown in blue in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#Fig1">Fig. 1b</a>). Grape chromosomes 1, 14 and 17 constitute a triplet having the same ancestor in common<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463–467 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d39870e2896">7</a></sup>. Chromosome segments with homologous genes common both to grape and apple (16 of a total of 18 comparisons) are indicated by gray boxes connected with dashed lines. Green, red and blue dots indicate increasing <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> values, in that order. Perpendicular lines on the <i>x</i> and <i>y</i> axes mark the middle of each chromosome. Green grid separates chromosomes.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.654/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The chromosome homologies derived from the recent GWD allow inference of the cytological events that have led to the number and composition of the extant apple chromosomes, starting from a putative nine-chromosome ancestor (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#Fig3">Fig. 3</a>). Each doublet of the eight apple chromosomes (3-11, 5-10, 9-17 and 13-16) is derived principally from one ancestor, although minor interchromosomal rearrangements have occurred (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#MOESM4">Supplementary Fig. 9a–k</a>). Chromosomes 4, 6, 12 and 14 originate from duplications of the ancient chromosomes V and VI, followed by a translocation and a deletion event. Similar events have generated chromosomes 1, 2, 7, 8 and 15 from chromosomes VII, VIII and IX. Chromosome 15 could have been produced from the translocation of an entire copy of chromosome IX into the centromeric region of chromosome VIII, following a model of dysploidy (reduction of chromosome number) common in cereals<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Luo, M.C. et al. Genome comparisons reveal a dominant mechanism of chromosome number reduction in grasses and accelerated genome evolution in Triticeae. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 106, 15780–15785 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d39870e2927">10</a></sup>. The second copy of ancient chromosome VIII has evolved into the extant chromosome 8. A conservative estimate of the number of large chromosome rearrangements since the divergence of the Pyreae subtribe, corresponding to the recent chromosome duplication, includes one chromosome fusion (extant chromosome 15), three translocations (involving extant chromosomes 1, 2 and 14), six deletions defined by telomeres that are not currently duplicated (chromosomes 4, 6, 8, 10, 11 and 13), one intrachromosome deletion (within chromosome 7, according to the chromosome 1–chromosome 7 comparison) and a deletion of a centromere (from ancient chromosome IX).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="A model explaining the evolution from a 9-chromosome ancestor to the 17-chromosome karyotype of extant Pyreae, including the genus Malus."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: A model explaining the evolution from a 9-chromosome ancestor to the 17-chromosome karyotype of extant Pyreae, including the genus <i>Malus</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.654/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.654/MediaObjects/41588_2010_Article_BFng654_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.654/MediaObjects/41588_2010_Article_BFng654_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="452"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>A GWD followed by a parsimony model of chromosome rearrangements is postulated. Shared colors indicate homology between extant chromosomes. White fragments of chromosomes indicate lack of a duplicated counterpart. The white-hatched portions of chromosomes 5 and 10 indicate partial homology (see also <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#MOESM4">Supplementary Fig. 9</a>). Black marks at chromosome ends correspond to those in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#Fig1">Figure 1b</a>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.654/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Molecular distances, taxonomy and phylogeny of Rosaceae</h3><p>Available Rosaceae molecular data allow intrafamily comparisons of apple with pear and of a consensus of apple and pear with peach. Further comparisons with grape—a species basal to rosids but belonging to the Vitaceae, a strictly different, although related, family—introduce the possibility of comparing interfamily molecular distances. DNA sequences used in this molecular phylogeny consist of those from EST databases and, for apple, the genomic data as described in detail in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#MOESM4">Supplementary Note</a>. Data from a three-way sequence alignment between predicted gene space in apple (<span class="stix">∼</span>84 Mb) and experimentally derived EST data from pear (<span class="stix">∼</span>14.9 Mb) and peach (<span class="stix">∼</span>18 Mb), performed as in ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Green, R.E. et al. Analysis of one million base pairs of Neanderthal DNA. Nature 444, 330–336 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d39870e2971">11</a>, indicates that the genetic distance, based on DNA sequence divergence per base pair between members of Rosaceae, increases from apple to pear to peach (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#MOESM4">Supplementary Table 20</a>). When predicted gene spaces of apple and pear were compared, a value of 96.35% nucleotide identity was calculated between these two species of the tribe Pyreae. The estimate for nucleotide identity between the tribes Pyreae and Amygdaleae (apple and peach) was 90.64%. When grape was compared with apple and pear, nucleotide identity was estimated at 85.31%. When the frequency of transitions and transversions was considered (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#Fig4">Fig. 4</a>), the ratio <i>R</i> (transitions/transversions) was similar for apple-specific and pear-specific mutations. For peach-specific mutations, the <i>R</i> value is more difficult to interpret, as it is probably biased by the existence of recent GWD in apple and pear. The comparison of apple and pear with grape showed that although transitions were only 20% more frequent than transversions, T-to-G transversions represented 12% of the total number of mutations observed (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#Fig4">Fig. 4d</a>), implying that Vitaceae is strongly divergent taxonomically from core members of the Rosaceae.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Molecular distances among Rosaceae species and their comparison with grape."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4: Molecular distances among Rosaceae species and their comparison with grape.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.654/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.654/MediaObjects/41588_2010_Article_BFng654_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.654/MediaObjects/41588_2010_Article_BFng654_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="430"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>–<b>c</b>) Mutations identified in a three-way comparison of apple, pear and peach. Numbers of transitions and transversions where apple (Ap) differs from pear (Pr) and peach (Pc) (<b>a</b>; 12,273 total), where pear differs from apple and peach (<b>b</b>; 13,124 total), and where peach differs from apple and pear (<b>c</b>; 381,619 total). (<b>d</b>) Number of transitions and transversions in a two-way alignment of grape DNA (Gr) to a consensus sequence (con.) of apple and pear (26,693 total). Note the high rate of T-to-G transversions. <i>R</i> is the transitions/transversions ratio. Methods and computer calculations were similar to those in ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Green, R.E. et al. Analysis of one million base pairs of Neanderthal DNA. Nature 444, 330–336 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d39870e3024">11</a> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#MOESM4">Supplementary Note</a>).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.654/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The granule-bound starch synthase (<i>gbss</i>) genes, also known as waxy (<i>Wx</i>) genes (divided in two groups, <i>Wx1</i> and <i>Wx2</i>), were also used<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Evans, R.C. &amp; Campbell, C.S. The origin of the apple subfamily (Maloideae; Rosaceae) is clarified by DNA sequence data from duplicated GBSSI genes. Am. J. Bot. 89, 1478–1484 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d39870e3054">4</a></sup> as a tool to study molecular taxonomy of Rosaceae (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#MOESM4">Supplementary Table 21</a>). We identified six <i>Wx</i> genes in the apple genome, located on chromosomes 7, 9 and 16 (<i>Wx1</i> type) and 8, 6 and 14 (<i>Wx2</i> type) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#MOESM4">Supplementary Fig. 10</a>). After counting <i>Wx</i> genes of apple, including putative gene losses in syntenic chromosomal segments, we were able to identify eight two-by-two syntenic regions containing or expected to contain <i>Wx</i> loci. If <i>Wx1-1</i> on chromosome 7 is not considered (because neither a syntenic <i>Wx-1-1</i> region nor a paralogous <i>Wx-1-1</i> copy was found ), four <i>Wx</i> loci should have been present in the nine-chromosome Pyreae ancestor, a result that is consistent with an ancestral paleopolyploid state. When the genomic <i>Wx</i> gene sequences were integrated in the phylogenetic analysis based on sequences present in the Rosaceae database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Potter, D. et al. Phylogeny and classification of Rosaceae. Plant Syst. Evol. 266, 5–43 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d39870e3097">12</a></sup>, the three <i>Wx-1</i> and the three <i>Wx-2</i> genes were mapped to two separate clades, both of which also included <i>Wx</i> genes of <i>Gillenia</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#MOESM4">Supplementary Fig. 11</a>). However, <i>Prunus</i> and <i>Spiraea</i> sequences clustered in separate clades, supporting the conclusions that the tight relationships between apple and <i>Gillenia Wx1</i> genes, as well as between apple and <i>Gillenia Wx2</i> genes, were probably generated by the recent GWD (the Pyreae event)—the founding step of the Pyreae genome—and that <i>Prunus</i>- and <i>Spiraea</i>-related species are less likely to have contributed to the Pyreae genome. Hence, we tested the Rosaceae molecular taxonomy<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Potter, D. et al. Phylogeny and classification of Rosaceae. Plant Syst. Evol. 266, 5–43 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d39870e3136">12</a></sup> by Bayesian analysis of the sequences of seven nuclear and chloroplast genes. A major clade with the maximum statistical support included all Pyreae (<i>x</i> = 17) as well as <i>Gillenia</i> (<i>x</i> = 9) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#MOESM4">Supplementary Fig. 12</a>). Notably, the genera <i>Spiraea</i> (<i>x</i> = 9) and <i>Prunus</i> (<i>x</i> = 8) were not included in this clade.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Apple domestication</h3><p>Although <i>M. sieversii</i> has been considered to be the ancestor of the domesticated apple<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Juniper, B.E. &amp; Mabberley, D.J. The Story of the Apple (Timber Press, Portland, Oregon, USA, 2006)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d39870e3176">13</a></sup>, this has been challenged by the identification of molecular similarities between domestic apple and <i>M. sylvestris</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Coart, E., Van Glabeke, S., De Loose, M., Larsen, A.S. &amp; Roldan-Ruiz, I. Chloroplast diversity in the genus Malus: new insights into the relationship between the European wild apple (Malus sylvestris (L.) Mill.) and the domesticated apple (Malus domestica Borkh.). Mol. Ecol. 15, 2171–2182 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d39870e3182">14</a></sup>. To test these two hypotheses, we surveyed molecular differences at 23 genes across the genus <i>Malus</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#MOESM4">Supplementary Table 22</a>). The 74 accessions we considered included 12 <i>M.</i> × <i>domestica</i> cultivars, 10 <i>M. sieversii</i>, 21 <i>M. sylvestris</i>, all major wild apple species and two <i>Pyrus</i> species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#MOESM4">Supplementary Table 23</a>). For <i>M.</i> × <i>domestica</i>, we included the cultivars 'Cox's Orange Pippin', 'Golden Delicious', 'McIntosh', 'Red Delicious' and 'Jonathan', the most important 'founders' of modern apple breeding<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Noiton, D.A.M. &amp; Alspach, P.A. Founding clones, inbreeding, coancestry, and status number of modern apple cultivars. J. Am. Soc. Hortic. Sci. 121, 773–782 (1996)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d39870e3218">15</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#MOESM4">Supplementary Note</a>). For each gene and accession, a PCR amplicon was resequenced and the data were analyzed as a concatenated data set with a total length of <span class="stix">∼</span>11,300 bp, with 1,507 polymorphic informative sites. A neighbor-net planar graph<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Huson, D.H. &amp; Bryant, D. Application of phylogenetic networks in evolutionary studies. Mol. Biol. Evol. 23, 254–267 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d39870e3225">16</a></sup> was constructed from the molecular differences among accessions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#Fig5">Fig. 5</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#MOESM4">Supplementary Fig. 13</a>). Although the clade containing <i>M. sylvestris</i> was well separated from the clade with <i>M.</i> × <i>domestica</i>, <i>M. sieversii</i> and <i>M.</i> × <i>domestica</i> genotypes shared a large common clade that also included accessions of <i>M. orientalis</i> and <i>M.</i> × <i>asiatica</i>. The average polymorphism rate within the domestic cultivars was 4.8 SNPs per kb, with 5.7 SNPs per kb between 'Golden Delicious' and <i>M. sieversii</i>, and 9.6 SNPs per kb between and <i>M. sylvestris</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#MOESM4">Supplementary Table 24</a>). The genetic differentiation was categorized as 'moderate' between <i>M.</i> × <i>domestica</i> and <i>M</i>. <i>sieversii</i> (<i>F</i><sub>st</sub> = 0.14), and 'great' between <i>M.</i> × <i>domestica</i> and <i>M. sylvestris</i> and between <i>M. sieversii</i> and <i>M. sylvestris</i> (<i>F</i><sub>st</sub> = 0.17 and <i>F</i><sub>st</sub> = 0.21, respectively)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Wright, S. Evolution and the Genetics of Populations Vol. 4 (University of Chicago Press, 1978)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d39870e3315">17</a></sup>. The mean numbers of haplotypes per gene were 6.4, 5.8 and 10.0 for <i>M.</i> × <i>domestica</i>, <i>M. sieversii</i> and <i>M. sylvestris</i>, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#MOESM4">Supplementary Table 25</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Phylogenetic relationships among Malus species, including M. × domestica cultivars, based on a multilocus concatenated sequence alignment derived from partial resequencing of 23 apple genetic loci."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 5: Phylogenetic relationships among <i>Malus</i> species, including <i>M.</i> × <i>domestica</i> cultivars, based on a multilocus concatenated sequence alignment derived from partial resequencing of 23 apple genetic loci.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.654/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.654/MediaObjects/41588_2010_Article_BFng654_Fig5_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.654/MediaObjects/41588_2010_Article_BFng654_Fig5_HTML.jpg" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="512"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>Black, orange and green, accessions of <i>M.</i> × <i>domestica</i> cultivars, <i>M. sieversii</i> and <i>M. sylvestris</i>, respectively; A, O and P, accessions of <i>M.</i> × <i>asiatica</i>, <i>M. orientalis</i> and <i>Pyrus</i>, respectively; squares, accessions of all other wild species. Full information on accessions is provided in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#MOESM4">Supplementary Table 23</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#MOESM4">Supplementary Figure 13</a>. The split separating the <i>M.</i> × <i>domestica</i>–<i>M. sieversii</i>–<i>M. orientalis–M.</i> × <i>asiatica</i> complex from other species is highlighted in red, and the split separating <i>M. sylvestris</i> is highlighted in green. Genetic distances were obtained as Hamming distances, with pairwise alignment of nucleotide positions. The planar graph was constructed with Splits-Tree 4.10.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.654/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec7" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec7-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec7">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec7-content"><p>The putative gene content in apple (57,386 putative genes plus 31,678 transposable element–related ORFs) is high compared to <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> (27,228), poplar (45,654), papaya (28,027), <i>Brachypodium distachyon</i> (25,532), grape (33,514), rice (40,577), sorghum (34,496), cucumber (26,682), soybean (46,430) and maize (32,540). Putative apple-specific genes, identified as described in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#MOESM4">Supplementary Note</a>, totaled 11,444. The gene density in apple (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#Tab2">Table 2</a>) is within the range of those in poplar and grape, but lower than those in <i>Arabidopsis</i>, <i>Brachypodium</i> and rice. The existence of hemizygous DNA in the heterozygous variety 'Golden Delicious' may have contributed to this gene number, as has also been noted for grape<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Velasco, R. et al. A high quality draft consensus sequence of the genome of a heterozygous grapevine variety. PLoS ONE 2, e1326 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d39870e3443">2</a></sup>.</p><p>The apple genome has a relatively high number of repeated sequences, which are difficult to assemble or anchor. As seen in grape and cereals, retrotransposons represent the most abundant transposable-element fraction, comprising 38% of the total genome and 89% of all transposable elements (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#Tab2">Table 2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#MOESM4">Supplementary Table 7</a>). In contrast, apple has the lowest content of DNA transposons (including the CACTA superfamily) among the reported plant genomes.</p><p>The number of transcription factors identified (4,021; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#MOESM4">Supplementary Table 7</a>) was among the highest of the sequenced plant genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#Tab2">Table 2</a>), although the allocation of transcription factor genes to gene families was similar to other sequenced plant species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#MOESM4">Supplementary Fig. 3</a>). Partial exceptions were the families <i>C2H2</i>, <i>CCAAT</i> and <i>NAC</i>, which were notably more represented in apple.</p><p>The fraction of nucleotide-binding site–leucine-rich repeat (<i>NBS-LRR</i>) resistance genes is considerably higher in eurosids II (apple, poplar and grape) than in eurosids I (<i>Arabidopsis</i>). In monocotyledons (rice), this class of genes predominates. The content of Toll/interleukin region (<i>TIR</i>)-<i>NBS-LRR</i> genes is highest in <i>Arabidopsis</i> (52%), lower in other eurosids (11–32%) and absent in monocots (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#Tab2">Table 2</a>). In addition to <i>NBS</i> genes, the apple genome contains 575 <i>LRR-kinase</i> genes.</p><p>As seen in other genomes, different classes of apple genes differ greatly in their degree of duplication (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#MOESM5">Supplementary Table 11</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#MOESM4">Supplementary Fig. 4</a>). Across the ten genomes considered, there are gene families with either low or high numbers of paralogous copies. This is particularly evident for genes likely to be involved in metabolism of anthocyanins and flavonoids, isoflavones and isoflavonones, and terpenes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#MOESM4">Supplementary Table 7</a>). Relevant cases in each pathway are <i>flavonone 3-hydroxylase</i> (2–13 copies in nine plant genomes) and <i>isoflavone reductase</i> (3–19 copies) compared to <i>isoflavone synthase</i> (54–151 copies); <i>squalene synthase</i> (13 copies) compared to <i>squalene monooxygenase</i> (1–27 copies). It seems that, for some gene classes, the number of paralogous copies may already have been established in the genome of common progenitor(s) of higher plants.</p><p>An intriguing aspect of the apple's biology concerns its characteristic fruit, the pome, which is found only in the Pyreae tribe<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Potter, D. et al. Phylogeny and classification of Rosaceae. Plant Syst. Evol. 266, 5–43 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d39870e3538">12</a></sup>. This indicates that the pome probably evolved after a relatively recent Pyreae-specific GWD, a polyploidization step that we hypothesize has contributed to the apple's developmental and metabolic specificity (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#MOESM4">Supplementary Table 7</a>). Pome fruit is derived by enlargement of the receptacle, which is the region below the whorl of sepals in the apple flower. <i>MADS-box</i> genes may regulate pome development, as they determine the eventual fate of floral tissues in all plant species analyzed so far<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Ng, M. &amp; Yanofsky, M.F. Activation of the Arabidopsis B class homeotic genes by APETALA1. Plant Cell 13, 739–753 (2001)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d39870e3548">18</a></sup>. For example, it has recently been shown that an apple <i>MADS-box</i> gene that is a member of the <i>AP1</i> clade, common to all flowering plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Shan, H. et al. Evolution of plant MADS Box transcription factors: evidence for shifts in selection associated with early angiosperm diversification and concerted gene duplications. Mol. Biol. Evol. 26, 2229–2244 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d39870e3559">19</a></sup> and closely related to <i>Arabidopsis FRUITFULL</i> (<i>FUL</i>), is differentially expressed during pome development<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Janssen, B.J. et al. Global gene expression analysis of apple fruit development from the floral bud to ripe fruit. BMC Plant Biol. 8, 16 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d39870e3569">20</a></sup>. In addition, a substantial number of apple type II <i>MADS</i>-<i>box</i> genes belong, phylogenetically, to the <i>StMADS11</i> subclade, a group named for its first reported member, which was isolated from potato (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#MOESM4">Supplementary Fig. 14a</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Becker, A. &amp; Theissen, G. The major clades of MADS-box genes and their role in the development and evolution of flowering plants. Mol. Phylogenet. Evol. 29, 464–489 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d39870e3586">21</a></sup>. This subclade includes only two <i>Arabidopsis</i> genes, <i>SVP</i> and <i>AGL24</i>. Ectopic overexpression of <i>SVP</i> and related genes in <i>Arabidopsis</i> leads to foliose sepal syndrome—that is, the formation of large sepals<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Masiero, S. et al. INCOMPOSITA: a MADS-box gene controlling prophyll development and floral meristem identity in Antirrhinum. Development 131, 5981–5990 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d39870e3606">22</a></sup>. In apple, this specific subclade not only includes two genes expressed in the pome but is also expanded to include 15 other genes.</p><p>Carbohydrate metabolism is another important aspect of fruit composition. In Rosaceae, photosynthesis-derived carbohydrates are transported mainly as sorbitol<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Newcomb, R.D. et al. Analyses of expressed sequence tags from apple. Plant Physiol. 141, 147–166 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d39870e3613">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Bieleski, R.L. Sugar alcohols. in Encyclopedia of Plant Physiology New Series Vol. 13 (eds. Loewus, F.A. &amp; Tanner, W.) 158–192 (Springer-Verlag, Berlin, 1982)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d39870e3616">24</a></sup>. Compared with other plant genomes, apple has considerably more copies of key genes related to sorbitol metabolism. These include <i>aldose 6-P reductase</i> (<i>A6PR</i>), which is rate-limiting for sorbitol biosynthesis, <i>sorbitol-dehydrogenase</i> (<i>SDH</i>), which converts sorbitol to fructose in the fruit<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Loescher, W.H., Marlow, G.C. &amp; Kennedy, R.A. Sorbitol metabolism and sink-source interconversions in developing apple leaves. Plant Physiol. 70, 335–339 (1982)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d39870e3633">25</a></sup>, and <i>sorbitol transporter PcSOT2</i>, which is specific to Rosaceae fruit<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Watari, J. et al. Identification of sorbitol transporters expressed in the phloem of apple source leaves. Plant Cell Physiol. 45, 1032–1041 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d39870e3640">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Gao, Z. et al. Cloning, expression, and characterization of sorbitol transporters from developing sour cherry fruit and leaf sink tissues. Plant Physiol. 131, 1566–1575 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d39870e3643">27</a></sup>. In total, there are 71 sorbitol metabolism genes in apple; in other species, the number ranges between 9 and 43 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#MOESM4">Supplementary Tables 7</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#MOESM4">26</a>, and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#MOESM4">Supplementary Fig. 14b–d</a>). In the Rosaceae, an evolutionary trend toward fruit organ specialization may have been partially based on gene duplication, which has created large families of specific paralogous genes (particularly evident for <i>SDH</i>; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#MOESM4">Supplementary Fig. 14c</a>). Gene families expanded in apple, such as <i>StMADS11</i>-like and <i>SDH</i>-like, have yet to be tested functionally for their involvement in fruit characteristics.</p><p>A number of models have been proposed to explain the uniquely high number of chromosomes in Pyreae, the most popular being the 'wide-hybridization' hypothesis based on an allopolyploidization event between spireoid (<i>x</i> = 9) and amygdaloid (<i>x</i> = 8) ancestors<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Chevreau, E., Lespinasse, Y. &amp; Gallet, M. Inheritance of pollen enzymes and polyploid origin of apple. (Malus x domestica Borkh.). Theor. Appl. Genet. 71, 268–277 (1985)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d39870e3678">28</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Phipps, J.B., Robertson, K.R., Rohrer, J.R. &amp; Smith, P.G. Origins and evolution of subfam. Maloideae (Rosaceae). Syst. Bot. 16, 303–332 (1991)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d39870e3681">29</a></sup>. More recent molecular phylogeny studies point to the possibility that Pyreae originated by autopolyploidization or by hybridization between two sister taxa with <i>x</i> = 9 (similar to extant <i>Gillenia</i>), followed by diploidization and aneuploidization<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Evans, R.C. &amp; Campbell, C.S. The origin of the apple subfamily (Maloideae; Rosaceae) is clarified by DNA sequence data from duplicated GBSSI genes. Am. J. Bot. 89, 1478–1484 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d39870e3692">4</a></sup> to <i>x</i> = 17. This hypothesis takes into account that <i>Gillenia</i> and related taxa are New World species and that the earliest fossil evidence of specimens belonging to extant genera of Pyreae are from North America.</p><p>Our results support the autopolyploidization hypothesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Evans, R.C. &amp; Campbell, C.S. The origin of the apple subfamily (Maloideae; Rosaceae) is clarified by DNA sequence data from duplicated GBSSI genes. Am. J. Bot. 89, 1478–1484 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d39870e3705">4</a></sup>, as the derivation from a <i>Gillenia</i>-like taxon best fits the available data. First, the apple genome derives from a relatively recent duplication. Relationships between its homologous chromosomes based on genome sequence extend observations based on synteny and collinearity of molecular markers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Maliepaard, C. et al. Aligning male and female linkage maps of apple (Malus pumila Mill.) using multi-allelic markers. Theor. Appl. Genet. 97, 60–73 (1998)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d39870e3712">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Celton, J.M., Tustin, D.S., Chagne, D. &amp; Gardiner, S.E. Construction of a dense genetic linkage map for apple rootstocks using SSRs developed from Malus ESTs and Pyrus genomic sequences. Tree Genet. Genomes 5, 93–107 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d39870e3715">31</a></sup>. The timing of such a GWD, as estimated from our genomic data (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#Fig1">Fig. 1c</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#MOESM4">Supplementary Figs. 15</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#MOESM4">16</a>), agrees with archeobotanical dates of 48–50 Mya<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Wolfe, J. &amp; Wehr, W.J.A. Rosaceous Chamaebatiaria-like foliage from the Paleogene of western North America. Aliso 12, 177–200 (1988)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d39870e3729">32</a></sup>.</p><p>Second, molecular phylogeny of <i>Wx</i> genes in the apple genome confirms the close relationship of <i>Gillenia</i> (<i>x</i> = 9) with the Pyreae (<i>x</i> = 17) lineage, as the <i>Wx</i> gene sequences of <i>Prunus</i>, <i>Spiraea</i> and other Rosaceae genera belong to a different phylogenetic cluster (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#MOESM4">Supplementary Fig. 11</a>). The monophyletic origin of Pyreae and <i>Gillenia</i> was confirmed by a molecular phylogeny of a broader set of genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#MOESM4">Supplementary Fig. 12</a>).</p><p>In addition, a simple and parsimonious pattern of chromosome breakage and fusion explains the derivation of the current <i>x</i> = 17 Pyreae karyotype from a polyploidization event of two <i>x</i> = 9 genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.654#Fig3">Fig. 3</a>). The rate of chromosome rearrangements after polyploidization (12 chromosome events in 60 My) is similar to that for poplar (<span class="stix">∼</span>16 events in 60 My)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Tuskan, G.A. et al. The genome of black cottonwood, Populus trichocarpa (Torr. &amp; Gray). Science 313, 1596–1604 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d39870e3780">6</a></sup> and lower than in maize (at least 17 chromosome fusion events in 5 My)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Salse, J. et al. Identification and characterization of shared duplications between rice and wheat provide new insight into grass genome evolution. Plant Cell 20, 11–24 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d39870e3784">33</a></sup> or in artificial neopolyploids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Doyle, J.J. et al. Evolutionary genetics of genome merger and doubling in plants. Annu. Rev. Genet. 42, 443–461 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d39870e3789">34</a></sup>. In this sense, molecular clocks of perennial woody species seem slower than those of annual species, in terms of both nucleotide substitutions and chromosome rearrangements<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Van de Peer, Y., Fawcett, J.A., Proost, S., Sterck, L. &amp; Vandepoele, K. The flowering world: a tale of duplications. Trends Plant Sci. 14, 680–688 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d39870e3793">9</a></sup>. For the genus <i>Helianthus</i>, a similar observation that only some of the ancestor chromosomes are rearranged in the extant chromosomes has been discussed in detail. In this genus, such rearrangement was associated with chromosomal differences between two sister species contributing to a GWD allopolyploid event<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Rieseberg, L.H., Sinervo, B., Linder, C.R., Ungerer, M.C. &amp; Arias, D.M. Role of gene interactions in hybrid speciation: evidence from ancient and experimental hybrids. Science 272, 741–745 (1996)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d39870e3800">35</a></sup>.</p><p>Similarly, the collinearity between <i>Pyrus</i> and <i>Malus</i> genetic maps<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Celton, J.M., Tustin, D.S., Chagne, D. &amp; Gardiner, S.E. Construction of a dense genetic linkage map for apple rootstocks using SSRs developed from Malus ESTs and Pyrus genomic sequences. Tree Genet. Genomes 5, 93–107 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d39870e3813">31</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Yamamoto, T. et al. Genetic linkage maps of Japanese and European pears aligned to the apple consensus map. Acta Hortic. 663, 51–56 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d39870e3816">36</a></sup> suggests that the Pyreae genome reorganization occurred before the divergence of the two genera. A rapid genome rearrangement after polyploidization is expected in species lacking the <i>Ph1</i>-like function that prevents the pairing of homologous chromosomes in wheat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Griffiths, S. et al. Molecular characterization of Ph1 as a major chromosome pairing locus in polyploid wheat. Nature 439, 749–752 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d39870e3823">37</a></sup>.</p><p>It has been proposed that central Asia is the center of origin of domesticated apple<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Vavilov, N.I. Wild progenitors of the fruit trees of Turkestan and the Caucasus and the problem of the origin of fruit trees. in Proceedings of the 9th International Horticultural Congress 271–286 (The Royal Horticultural Society, London, 1930)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d39870e3830">38</a></sup>. Between 25 and 47 different <i>Malus</i> species, including <i>M.</i> × <i>domestica</i>, are currently recognized<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Robinson, J.P., Harris, S.A. &amp; Juniper, B.E. Taxonomy of the genus Malus Mill. (Rosaceae) with emphasis on the cultivated apple, Malus domestica Borkh. Plant Syst. Evol. 226, 35–58 (2001)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d39870e3843">39</a></sup>. As asiatic <i>M.</i> × <i>asiatica, M. baccata, M. micromalus, M. orientalis, M. prunifolia</i> and <i>M. sieversii</i>, and European <i>M. sylvestris</i>, are the species taxonomically closest to <i>M.</i> × <i>domestica</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Robinson, J.P., Harris, S.A. &amp; Juniper, B.E. Taxonomy of the genus Malus Mill. (Rosaceae) with emphasis on the cultivated apple, Malus domestica Borkh. Plant Syst. Evol. 226, 35–58 (2001)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d39870e3866">39</a></sup>, they are considered to have contributed, to differing extents, to the domestic gene pool. <i>M. sieversii</i>, common in the Tian Shan region of central Asia, is the only wild species sharing all the qualities of the domesticated apple in terms of fruit and tree morphology<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Forsline, P.L., Aldwinckle, H.S., Dickson, E.E., Luby, J.J. &amp; Hokanson, S.C. Collection, maintenance, characterization, and utilization of wild apples of Central Asia. Hortic. Rev. (Am. Soc. Hortic. Sci.) 29, 1–61 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d39870e3873">40</a></sup>.</p><p>Apples are known to have been gathered in the Neolithic and Bronze Age in the Near East and Europe, and all archaeological findings indicate a fruit size compatible with those of the wild <i>M. sylvestris</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Zohary, D. &amp; Hopf, M. Domestication of Plants in the Old World: The Origin and Spread of Cultivated Plants in West Asia, Europe and the Nile Valley (Clarendon Press, Oxford, 1994)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d39870e3882">41</a></sup>, a species bearing small astringent and acidulate fruits. Sweet apples corresponding to extant domestic apples appeared in the Near East around 4,000 years ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Zohary, D. &amp; Hopf, M. Domestication of Plants in the Old World: The Origin and Spread of Cultivated Plants in West Asia, Europe and the Nile Valley (Clarendon Press, Oxford, 1994)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d39870e3886">41</a></sup>, at the time when the grafting technology used to propagate the highly heterozygous and self-incompatible apple was becoming available. From the Middle East, the domesticated apple passed to the Greeks and Romans, who spread fruit cultivation across Europe<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Juniper, B.E. &amp; Mabberley, D.J. The Story of the Apple (Timber Press, Portland, Oregon, USA, 2006)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d39870e3890">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Zohary, D. &amp; Hopf, M. Domestication of Plants in the Old World: The Origin and Spread of Cultivated Plants in West Asia, Europe and the Nile Valley (Clarendon Press, Oxford, 1994)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d39870e3893">41</a></sup>.</p><p>On the basis of our molecular results, <i>M.</i> × <i>domestica</i> cultivars appear more closely related to accessions of the wild species <i>M. sieversii</i> and less closely related to accessions of <i>M. sylvestris</i>, <i>M. baccata</i>, <i>M. micromalus</i> and <i>M. prunifolia</i>. The already known<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Gharghani, A. et al. Genetic identity and relationships of Iranian apple (Malus x domestica Borkh.) cultivars and landraces, wild Malus species and representative old apple cultivars based on simple sequence repeat (SSR) marker analysis. Genet. Resour. Crop Evol. 56, 829–842 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d39870e3922">42</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Luby, J. Taxonomy, classification and brief history. in Apples: Botany, Production and Uses (eds. Ferree, D.C. &amp; Warrington, I.J.) 1–14 (CABI, Cambridge, Massachusetts, USA, 2003)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d39870e3925">43</a></sup> genetic similarity of <i>M. sieversii</i> to <i>M. orientalis</i> and to <i>M.</i> × <i>asiatica</i> (a Chinese cultivated apple form) is also confirmed by our data.</p><p>The data support the formation of the <i>M.</i> × <i>domestica</i> gene pool from <i>M. sieversii</i>. Once grafting was introduced, the crop passed through a process described as 'instant domestication'<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Zohary, D. &amp; Spiegelroy, P. Beginnings of fruit growing in the Old World. Science 187, 319–327 (1975)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d39870e3954">44</a></sup>. This could explain apple's lack of domestication syndrome, which is the loss of sexual reproduction, seed dispersion and seed dormancy. Despite evidence of intrageneric hybridizations<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Coart, E., Van Glabeke, S., De Loose, M., Larsen, A.S. &amp; Roldan-Ruiz, I. Chloroplast diversity in the genus Malus: new insights into the relationship between the European wild apple (Malus sylvestris (L.) Mill.) and the domesticated apple (Malus domestica Borkh.). Mol. Ecol. 15, 2171–2182 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d39870e3958">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Korban, S.S. Interspecific hybridization in Malus. HortScience 21, 41–48 (1986)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d39870e3961">45</a></sup>, the possibility of substantial genetic contributions to the domestic gene pool of other wild <i>Malus</i> species, such as <i>M. sylvestris</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Coart, E., Van Glabeke, S., De Loose, M., Larsen, A.S. &amp; Roldan-Ruiz, I. Chloroplast diversity in the genus Malus: new insights into the relationship between the European wild apple (Malus sylvestris (L.) Mill.) and the domesticated apple (Malus domestica Borkh.). Mol. Ecol. 15, 2171–2182 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.654#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d39870e3971">14</a></sup>, was rejected in our analysis.</p><p>Our study also fully supports the proposal that <i>M.</i> × <i>domestica</i> and <i>M. sieversii</i> are the same species, for which the more appropriate nomenclature of <i>M. pumila</i> Mill. could be adopted<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Juniper, B.E. &amp; Mabberley, D.J.
